TITLE: Use of Temporary Immersion Bioreactors for Secondary Metabolite Production
URL: https://plantmethods.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z

        <title>Use of a temporary immersion bioreactor system for the sustainable production of thapsigargin in shoot cultures of Thapsia garganica | Plant Methods | Full Text</title>

        
    
    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Thapsigargin and nortrilobolide are sesquiterpene lactones found in the Mediterranean plant Thapsia garganica L. Thapsigargin is a potent inhibitor of the sarco/endoplasmic reticulum calcium ATPase pump, inducing apoptosis in mammalian cells. This mechanism has been used to develop a thapsigargin-based cancer drug first by GenSpera and later Inspyr Therapeutics (Westlake Village, California). However, a stable production of thapsigargin is not established. In vitro regeneration from leaf explants, shoot multiplication and rooting of T. garganica was obtained along with the production of thapsigargins in temporary immersion bioreactors (TIBs). Thapsigargin production was enhanced using reduced nutrient supply in combination with methyl jasmonate elicitation treatments. Shoots grown in vitro were able to produce 0.34% and 2.1% dry weight of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide, respectively, while leaves and stems of wild T. garganica plants contain only between 0.1 and 0.5% of thapsigargin and below detectable levels of nortrilobolide. In addition, a real-time reverse transcription PCR (qRT-PCR) study was performed to study the regulatory role of the biosynthetic genes HMG-CoA reductase (HMGR), farnesyl diphosphate synthase (FPPS), epikunzeaol synthase (TgTPS2) and the cytochrome P450 (TgCYP76AE2) of stem, leaf and callus tissues. Nadi staining showed that the thapsigargins are located in secretory ducts within these tissues. Shoot regeneration, rooting and biomass growth from leaf explants of T. garganica were achieved, together with a high yield in vitro production of thapsigargin in TIBs."/>

    <link rel="canonical" href="https://plantmethods.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z"/>
    

        
        
        
    
    <meta property="og:url" content="https://plantmethods.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z"/>
    <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
    <meta property="og:site_name" content="BioMed Central"/>
    <meta property="og:title" content="Use of a temporary immersion bioreactor system for the sustainable production of thapsigargin in shoot cultures of Thapsia garganica - Plant Methods"/>
    <meta property="og:description" content="Background Thapsigargin and nortrilobolide are sesquiterpene lactones found in the Mediterranean plant Thapsia garganica L. Thapsigargin is a potent inhibitor of the sarco/endoplasmic reticulum calcium ATPase pump, inducing apoptosis in mammalian cells. This mechanism has been used to develop a thapsigargin-based cancer drug first by GenSpera and later Inspyr Therapeutics (Westlake Village, California). However, a stable production of thapsigargin is not established. Results In vitro regeneration from leaf explants, shoot multiplication and rooting of T. garganica was obtained along with the production of thapsigargins in temporary immersion bioreactors (TIBs). Thapsigargin production was enhanced using reduced nutrient supply in combination with methyl jasmonate elicitation treatments. Shoots grown in vitro were able to produce 0.34% and 2.1% dry weight of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide, respectively, while leaves and stems of wild T. garganica plants contain only between 0.1 and 0.5% of thapsigargin and below detectable levels of nortrilobolide. In addition, a real-time reverse transcription PCR (qRT-PCR) study was performed to study the regulatory role of the biosynthetic genes HMG-CoA reductase (HMGR), farnesyl diphosphate synthase (FPPS), epikunzeaol synthase (TgTPS2) and the cytochrome P450 (TgCYP76AE2) of stem, leaf and callus tissues. Nadi staining showed that the thapsigargins are located in secretory ducts within these tissues. Conclusions Shoot regeneration, rooting and biomass growth from leaf explants of T. garganica were achieved, together with a high yield in vitro production of thapsigargin in TIBs."/>

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Use of a temporary immersion bioreactor system for the sustainable production of thapsigargin in shoot cultures of Thapsia garganica","description":"Thapsigargin and nortrilobolide are sesquiterpene lactones found in the Mediterranean plant Thapsia garganica L. Thapsigargin is a potent inhibitor of the sarco/endoplasmic reticulum calcium ATPase pump, inducing apoptosis in mammalian cells. This mechanism has been used to develop a thapsigargin-based cancer drug first by GenSpera and later Inspyr Therapeutics (Westlake Village, California). However, a stable production of thapsigargin is not established. In vitro regeneration from leaf explants, shoot multiplication and rooting of T. garganica was obtained along with the production of thapsigargins in temporary immersion bioreactors (TIBs). Thapsigargin production was enhanced using reduced nutrient supply in combination with methyl jasmonate elicitation treatments. Shoots grown in vitro were able to produce 0.34% and 2.1% dry weight of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide, respectively, while leaves and stems of wild T. garganica plants contain only between 0.1 and 0.5% of thapsigargin and below detectable levels of nortrilobolide. In addition, a real-time reverse transcription PCR (qRT-PCR) study was performed to study the regulatory role of the biosynthetic genes HMG-CoA reductase (HMGR), farnesyl diphosphate synthase (FPPS), epikunzeaol synthase (TgTPS2) and the cytochrome P450 (TgCYP76AE2) of stem, leaf and callus tissues. Nadi staining showed that the thapsigargins are located in secretory ducts within these tissues. Shoot regeneration, rooting and biomass growth from leaf explants of T. garganica were achieved, together with a high yield in vitro production of thapsigargin in                         <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="">Use of a temporary immersion bioreactor system for the sustainable production of thapsigargin in shoot cultures of <i>Thapsia garganica</i></h1>
                        <ul class="c-article-author-list c-article-author-list--short" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li href="#auth-Karen-Martinez_Swatson-Aff4" data-author-popup="auth-Karen-Martinez_Swatson-Aff4" data-author-search="Martinez-Swatson, Karen">Karen Martinez-Swatson</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a                         <p class="c-article-info-details" data-container-section="info">

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Thapsigargin and nortrilobolide are sesquiterpene lactones found in the Mediterranean plant <i>Thapsia garganica</i> L. Thapsigargin is a potent inhibitor of the sarco/endoplasmic reticulum calcium ATPase pump, inducing apoptosis in mammalian cells. This mechanism has been used to develop a thapsigargin-based cancer drug first by GenSpera and later Inspyr Therapeutics (Westlake Village, California). However, a stable production of thapsigargin is not established.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>In vitro regeneration from leaf explants, shoot multiplication and rooting of <i>T. garganica</i> was obtained along with the production of thapsigargins in temporary immersion bioreactors (TIBs). Thapsigargin production was enhanced using reduced nutrient supply in combination with methyl jasmonate elicitation treatments. Shoots grown in vitro were able to produce 0.34% and 2.1% dry weight of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide, respectively, while leaves and stems of wild <i>T. garganica</i> plants contain only between 0.1 and 0.5% of thapsigargin and below detectable levels of nortrilobolide. In addition, a real-time reverse transcription PCR (qRT-PCR) study was performed to study the regulatory role of the biosynthetic genes HMG-CoA reductase (<i>HMGR</i>), farnesyl diphosphate synthase (<i>FPPS</i>), epikunzeaol synthase (<i>TgTPS2</i>) and the cytochrome P450 (<i>TgCYP76AE2</i>) of stem, leaf and callus tissues. Nadi staining showed that the thapsigargins are located in secretory ducts within these tissues.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>Shoot regeneration, rooting and biomass growth from leaf explants of <i>T. garganica</i> were achieved, together with a high yield in vitro production of thapsigargin in TIBs.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Thapsigargins are sesquiterpene lactones that are widespread within the Mediterranean genus <i>Thapsia</i> (Apiaceae) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Simonsen HT, Weitzel C, Christensen SB. Guaianolide sesquiterpenoids: their pharmacology and biosynthesis. In: Ramawat KG, Merillon JM, editors. Handbook of natural products: phytochemistry, botany and metabolism of alkaloids, phenolics and terpenes. Berlin: Springer; 2013. p. 306998." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e486">1</a>]. Among this family of natural compounds, thapsigargin and nortrilobolide are present in the species <i>Thapsia garganica</i> and <i>Thapsia gymnesica</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Weitzel C, Rnsted N, Spalik K, Simonsen HT. Resurrecting deadly carrots: towards a revision of Thapsia (Apiaceae) based on phylogenetic analysis of nrITS sequences and chemical profiles. Bot J Linn Soc. 2014;174:62036." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e495">2</a>]. Thapsigargins are potent inhibitors of the mammalian sarco/endoplasmic reticulum Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase (SERCA) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shoel H, Jensen AML, Mller JV, Nissen P, Denmeade SR, Isaacs JT, Olsen CE, Christensen SB. Natural products as starting materials for development of second-generation SERCA inhibitors targeted towards prostate cancer cells. Bioorg Med Chem. 2006;14:28105. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.bmc.2005.12.001&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e501">3</a>]. When the molecules bind to the transmembrane of mammalian cells, SERCA is prevented from maintaining a low concentration of Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the cytosol and a high Ca<sup>2+</sup> concentration in the reticulum leading to apoptosis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shoel H, Jensen AML, Mller JV, Nissen P, Denmeade SR, Isaacs JT, Olsen CE, Christensen SB. Natural products as starting materials for development of second-generation SERCA inhibitors targeted towards prostate cancer cells. Bioorg Med Chem. 2006;14:28105. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.bmc.2005.12.001&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e508">3</a>]. A subnanomolar affinity for SERCA has made thapsigargin the most intensely studied molecule of the family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Andersen TB, Lopez CQ, Manczak T, Martinez KA, Simonsen HT. Thapsigarginfrom Thapsia L. to Mipsagargin. Molecules. 2015;20:611327. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/molecules20046113&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e511">4</a>].</p><p>Due to its ability to kill mammalian cells, thapsigargin has been used as a therapeutic target to induce apoptosis in cancer cells with a low rate of cell proliferation, thus with resistance to standard anti-proliferative chemotherapy. To target thapsigargin to the cancer cells, thapsigargin is fused with a masking peptide, which inhibits its biological activity until proteolytic cleavage at the tumor site [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Doan NT, Paulsen ES, Sehgal P, Mller JV, Nissen P, Denmeade SR, Isaacs JT, Dionne CA, Christensen SB. Targeting thapsigargin towards tumors. Steroids. 2015;97:27. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.steroids.2014.07.009&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e517">5</a>]. Based on this technology, InSpyr Therapeutics (Westlake Village, California, previously GenSpera) has patented a thapsigargin-based prodrug named Mipsagargin (G-202). Mipsagargin has been studied in a Phase 2 clinical trial in patients with hepatocellular carcinoma [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Doan NTQ, Christensen SB. Thapsigargin, origin, chemistry, structure-activity relationships and prodrug development. Curr Pharm Des. 2015;21:550117. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.2174/1381612821666151002112824&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e520">6</a>], but the current status of the trials is unclear.</p><p>Marketing of a thapsigargin-based drug will increase the demand for this molecule. Until now, all of the commercially available thapsigargin is obtained from fruits and roots of wild <i>T. garganica</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Andersen TB, Lopez CQ, Manczak T, Martinez KA, Simonsen HT. Thapsigarginfrom Thapsia L. to Mipsagargin. Molecules. 2015;20:611327. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/molecules20046113&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e529">4</a>]. Traditional field cultivation techniques are very difficult and not economically viable for <i>T. garganica,</i> as with many species of Apiaceae, since the plants are difficult to germinate from seeds [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ekiert H. Medicinal plant biotechnology: the Apiaceae family as the example of rapid development. Pharmazie. 2000;55:5617." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e535">7</a>]. ThapsIbiza (Islas Baleares, Spain) is the only company that has started a small production of <i>T. garganica</i> plants. On the other hand, the total synthesis of thapsigargin was described in 2007. The approach allowed the total synthesis of thapsigargin in 42 steps from (s)-carvone with an overall yield of 0.6% [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Andrews SP, Ball M, Wierschem F, Cleator E, Oliver S, Hogenauer K, Simic O, Antonello A, Hunger U, Smith MD, Ley SV. Total synthesis of five thapsigargins: guaianolide natural products exhibiting sub-nanomolar SERCA inhibition. Chem A Eur J. 2007;13:5688712." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e542">8</a>], and recent developments have cut this to 12 steps with a 5.8% yield [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen D, Evans PA. A concise, efficient and scalable total synthesis of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide from (R)-()-carvone. J Am Chem Soc. 2017;139:60469. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1021/jacs.7b01734&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e545">9</a>] and 11 steps with an overall yield of 0.137% [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chu H, Smith JM, Felding J, Baran PS. Scalable synthesis of ()-thapsigargin. ACS Cent Sci. 2017;3:4751. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1021/acscentsci.6b00313&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e548">10</a>]. Despite the successful synthesis of thapsigargin, the many steps for obtaining the core of the structure and the cost of the starting material make this approach commercially challenging. The biosynthesis of thapsigargins is not established and thus heterologous production in e.g. yeast or mosses is not yet possible [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Andersen TB, Lopez CQ, Manczak T, Martinez KA, Simonsen HT. Thapsigarginfrom Thapsia L. to Mipsagargin. Molecules. 2015;20:611327. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/molecules20046113&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e551">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pickel B, Drew DP, Manczak T, Weitzel C, Simonsen HT, Ro D. Identification and characterization of a kunzeaol synthase from Thapsia garganica: implications for the biosynthesis of the pharmaceutical thapsigargin. Biochem J. 2012;448:26171. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1042/bj20120654&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e554">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Andersen TB, Martinez KA, Rasmussen SA, Boughton BA, Jrgensen K, Andersen-Ranberg J, Nyberg N, Christensen SB, Simonsen HT. Localization and in vivo characterization of Thapsia garganica CYP76AE2 indicates a role in thapsigargin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:5672. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.16.00055&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e557">12</a>].</p><p>Plant tissue culture technologies can provide an alternative production platform of thapsigargins and ensure a stable supply of thapsigargin at an industrial scale, using the temporary immersion bioreactors (TIB) developed at Alkion Biopharma (now part of Evonik Botanicals) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Michoux F, Ahmad N, Hennig A, Nixon PJ, Warzecha H. Production of leafy biomass using temporary immersion bioreactors: an alternative platform to express proteins in transplastomic plants with drastic phenotypes. Planta. 2013;237:9038. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00425-012-1829-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e563">13</a>] and is a prerequisite for plant transformation. TIBs have many advantages regarding other conventional in vitro plant propagation techniques. TIBs reduce manual labor, the risk of contamination and therefore the cost of the process. TIBs also avoid asphyxiation and tissue vitrification by exposing the plants to the liquid medium with periodic immersions, which ensures a complete renewal of the atmosphere. Plant growth and development can be controlled by modifying the frequency and duration of the immersion.</p><p>Only a few reports have been published on in vitro cultures of <i>T. garganica</i> since the first in 1993 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jger A, Schottlnder B, Smitt U, Nyman U. Somatic embryogenesis in cell cultures of Thapsia garganica. Plant Cell Rep. 1993;12:51720. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/bf00236099&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e573">14</a>]. A cell culture was established in which somatic embryos were spontaneously formed and followed by the development of roots and shoots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jger A, Schottlnder B, Smitt U, Nyman U. Somatic embryogenesis in cell cultures of Thapsia garganica. Plant Cell Rep. 1993;12:51720. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/bf00236099&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e576">14</a>]. However, only nortrilobolide and trilobolid were found in the tissue cultures obtained in this study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jger A, Schottlnder B, Smitt U, Nyman U. Somatic embryogenesis in cell cultures of Thapsia garganica. Plant Cell Rep. 1993;12:51720. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/bf00236099&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e579">14</a>]. Later, Makunga et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Makunga NP, Jger AK, van Staden J. Micropropagation of Thapsia garganica: a medicinal plant. Plant Cell Rep. 2003;21:96773. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00299-003-0623-8&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e582">15</a>] published protocols for the micropropagation of <i>T. garganica</i> and acclimation of ex vitro plants. This was followed by the in vitro regeneration of <i>T. garganica</i> from leaf explants via direct organogenesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Makunga NP, Jger AK, van Staden J. An improved system for the in vitro regeneration of Thapsia garganica via direct organogenesis: influence of auxins and cytokinins. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2005;82:27180." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e592">16</a>] and with improved rooting and hyperhydricity in regenerating tissues [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Makunga NP, Jger AK, van Staden J. Improved in vitro rooting and hyperhydricity in regenerating tissues of Thapsia garganica L. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2006;86:7786." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e595">17</a>]. Nonetheless, thapsigargin was not detected or measured in these studies. Thus, to the best of our knowledge thapsigargin production has not been established in in vitro cultures prior to this work. The main objective of this study was to establish and evaluate an efficient production platform of thapsigargin based on in vitro tissue culture techniques. We established in vitro shoot cultures that can be cultivated at an industrial scale in TIBs. The cultures ability to produce thapsigargin and nortrilobolide was quantified by analytical chemistry techniques (HPLC and UPLC-MS) along with the gene expression (qRT-PCR) of the biosynthetic genes: <i>HMGR</i>, <i>FPPS</i>, <i>TgTPS2</i> and <i>TgCYP76AE2</i>. Analysis with Nadi staining was also performed in the tissue cultures.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">In vitro culture establishment</h3><p>Among the different treatments that were applied to leaf explants, only treatments combining auxin (2,4-Dichlorophenoxyacetic acid (2,4-D)) along with cytokinins (1-phenyl-3-(1,2,3-thiadiazol-5-yl) urea (TDZ) or N-(Phenylmethyl)-7H-purin-6-amine (BAP)) responded positively. From these, somatic embryogenesis and calli development were quantified (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Tab1">1</a>). Direct and indirect shoot regeneration was observed in some of the cultures (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig1">1</a>a), but no significant statistical differences between different concentrations of combined treatments were noted.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table1 The effect of different plant growth regulators on the induction of callus and somatic embryos from leaf explants of <i>T. garganica</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/tables/1" aria-label="Full size table 1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig.1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="442"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>T. garganica</i> leaf explants after 12weeks in MS with different plant growth regulators (plant growth regulators). <b>a</b> TDZ (0.1mg/L)+2,4-D (0.1mg/L), 16h light photoperiod yielding calli type I (3) and III (4), and presence of embryos in different stages (heart (1) and torpedo (2) stages) and shoot buds (1). <b>b</b> BAP (0.1mg/L)+2,4-D (0.1mg/L), 16h light photoperiod yielding the presence of embryos (1) and a shoot (5). <b>c</b> TDZ (1mg/L)+2,4-D (0.1mg/L), 16h light photoperiod yielding calli type I (3) and III (4), and presence the of shoot bud (5). <b>d</b> TDZ (0.5mg/L)+2,4-D (0.1mg/L), darkness conditions yielding calli type I and II (6) and the presence of embryos (1). plant growth regulator</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<p>The number of embryos produced per leaf explant varied with the concentration of cytokinins applied. Treatments with the lowest hormone concentrations, 2,4-D (0.1mg/L)+TDZ or BAP (0.1mg/L) yielded the highest number of somatic embryogenesis (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Tab1">1</a>). However, low concentrations of auxin 2,4-D or cytokinins (TDZ or BAP) alone, did not induce any organogenic response (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Tab1">1</a>). The photoperiod also influenced somatic embryogenesis. Leaf explants in total darkness seemed to produce more somatic embryos than explants grown in light. The continuous dark treatment, TDZ or BAP (0.1mg/L)+2,4-D (0.1mg/L) produced the highest percentage of explants forming embryos (83.3%) and the highest number of embryos per treatment (31.0%).</p><p>Explants in media containing TDZ alone and under light had low callus induction frequency (1043.3%), which increased with increasing TDZ concentration (0.11mg/L). The callus formed under these treatments did not proliferate and was classified as type IV brown dead calli. The combined treatments with cytokinins and auxin resulted in high callus formation in the explant cut surfaces. Leaf tissue cultured in BAP+2,4-D showed lower percentages of explant forming callus (63-96%) in comparison with leaf tissue cultured in TDZ+2,4-D (96100%). Additionally, callus morphology was significantly influenced by the light regimen (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig1">1</a>bd). Both light regimens gave rise to friable soft white calli (type I) identified as embryogenic calli. Explants under dark condition formed creamy yellow nodular calli defined as type II while explants under light conditions formed nodular green calli (type III).</p><p>The presence of shoots was also as observed, with no significant statistical difference between the different treatments, although a high concentration of BAP or TDZ tended to induce hyperhydricity of the shoots. This tendency requires further studies to confirm if this can be used for faster shoot induction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Rooting</h3><p>Rooting occurred in all tested treatments, reaching 4971% of rooted shoots after 6weeks (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig2">2</a>a) (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Tab2">2</a>). The number of roots increased with increased concentrations of auxin. However, in treatments with the highest auxin concentration, the shoot systems were greatly damaged or died due to stress. Therefore, treatments with the lowest auxin concentrations were more suitable for rooting.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig.2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="253"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro plant after root induction treatment (MS+IAA (4mg/L)) from a culture tube. <b>b</b>, <b>c</b> <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro shoots in TIB (temporary immersion bioreactor). <b>d</b> RITA<sup></sup> operation cycle: (1) Plants are placed on a fixed raft support in the upper compartment, and the culture medium is in the lower compartment (200mL). (2) An overpressure of 30kPa of sterile air is applied in the lower compartment, which pushes the medium into the upper compartment, immersing the explants. (3) During 3min every 6h, a sterile airflow continuously agitates and oxygenates the medium allowing an inner atmosphere renewal. (4) The airflow is stopped and the culture medium drains into the lower compartment by gravity</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table2 In vitro rooting of <i>T. garganica</i> shoots on MS medium supplemented with different concentration of IAA, NAA and IBA after 6weeks</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/tables/2" aria-label="Full size table 2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Production of thapsigargins in the rooting experiment</h3><p>A high-performance liquid chromatography (HPLC) analysis of extracts of the plant cultures showed different concentrations of thapsigargin depending on the treatment and tissue type (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Tab2">2</a>). The highest amounts of thapsigargin (3.77mg/g DW (dry weight)) and nortrilobolide (13.08mg/g DW) were obtained in shoots cultivated in MS medium without plant growth regulators. The level of thapsigargin was significant for this medium, whereas for nortrilobolide it was not. This treatment did not provide any rooting since there were no plant growth regulators. This initiated the work in TIBs, where rooting is not a prerequisite.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Production of thapsigargins in TIBs</h3><p><i>Thapsia garganica</i> in vitro shoots were grown in TIBs for the mass production of thapsigargins (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig2">2</a>b). The shoot cultures were grown with the following elicitors: cellulase, alginate, yeast extract and MeJA; and the inhibitor miconazole (data not shown). Only the MeJA elicitor stimulated the production of thapsigargins (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig3">3</a>). Thapsigargin and notrilobolide contents were quantified by HPLC. An increase in thapsigargin and nortrilobolide was observed throughout the growth period and increased with increasing MeJA concentration. After 18days of growth with 400M MeJA treatments, 2.15mg/g DW of thapsigargin and 17.42mg/g DW of nortrilobolide were obtained. Only the increase in nortrilobolide concentration was statistically significant (<i>p</i>&lt;0.0; F-value=16.2).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig.3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="237"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The effect of MeJA elicitation on thapsigargin and nortrilobolide content (mg/g DW) in <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro shoots in TIBs. The medium was MS salts and vitamins supplemented with 1.5mg/L BAP and 0.5mg/L NAA and different concentrations of MeJA (100, 200 and 400M). An asterisk (*) indicates a significant difference between the sample and the control. This is based on an ANOVA test followed by a Tukeys multiple comparison test (<i>p</i>0.05). n=69 samples per treatment.TG=thapsigarginshown in (<b>a</b>), NT=nortrilobolideshown in (<b>b</b>), DW=dry weight</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Another study was performed with MeJA elicitation combined with reduced nutrient supply (MS) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig4">4</a>). With this treatment, the amount of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide reached 3.370.23mg/g DW and 21.501.87mg/g DW. This represents a 2.6 and 2.1 fold increase (<i>p</i>&lt;0.0; F-values=32.6 and 21.06 respectively) and a total of 2.49% of the dry weight constitute these two molecules alone. For this experiment, we also observed that during the 18days the TIBs biomass increased 3.4 fold for the control, 1.6 fold for the MS and 1.5 fold for MS+MeJA. The natural content of thapsigargins has been reported up to 0.5% in the stem and leaf. Thus, 2.49% (DW) is a very promising result.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig.4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="232"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The effect of reduced nutrient supply; either alone or in combination with MeJA elicitation, on thapsigargin and nortrilobolide content (mg/g DW) in <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro shoots grown 18days in TIBs. As controls TIBs with MS medium supplemented with BAP (1.5mg/L) and NAA (0.5mg/L) were used. Different letters on the bars indicate significant differences based on an ANOVA test followed by a Tukeys multiple comparison test (<i>p</i>0.05). n=9 bioreactors per treatment. TG=thapsigarginshown in (<b>a</b>), NT=nortrilobolideshown in (<b>b</b>), DW=dry weight</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Identification of thapsigargins</h3><p>In order to identify the major thapsigargins, a UPLC-MS analysis was performed. Five major peaks were separated and identified through UPLC-MS (Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig5">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig6">6</a>) and the mass spectra were compared with previously published data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Christensen SB, Norup E, Rasmussen U, Madsen J. Structure of histamine releasing guaianolides from Thapsia species. Phytochemistry. 1984;23:165963." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2571">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu HZ, Jensen KG, Tran LM, Chen M, Zhai L, Olsen CE, Shoel H, Denmeade SR, Isaacs JT, Christensen SB. Cytotoxic phenylpropanoids and an additional thapsigargin analogue isolated from Thapsia garganica. Phytochemistry. 2006;67:26518. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.phytochem.2006.10.005&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2571_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Christensen SB, Norup E. Absolute configurations of the histamine liberating sesquiterpene lactones thapsigargin and trilobolide. Tetrahedron Lett. 1985;26:10710." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2571_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rasmussen U, Christensen SB, Sandberg F. Thapsigargine and thapsigargicine, two new histamine liberators from Thapsia garganica L. Acta Pharm Suec. 1978;15:13340." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2574">21</a>]. The mass spectrum of peak A corresponds to nortrilobolide (MW=508.5) (NCBI, PubChem CID=10097774), peak B corresponds to thapsivillosin I (MW=605.4) (NCBI, PubChem CID=102157434), peak D corresponds to thapsigargin (MW=650.7) (NCBI, PubChem CID=446378) and the MS spectrum peak E correspond to thapsivillosin C (MW=663.5). The spectrum of peak C could not be identified. These results confirmed the presence of these molecules in in vitro cultures of <i>T</i>. <i>garganica</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig.5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="454"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>UV absorption chromatogram from a UPLC analysis of extracts of <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro plants grown in TIBs. The chromatogram shows the signal at 230nm. (<i>a</i>) nortrilobolide, (<i>b</i>) thapsivillosin I, (<i>d</i>) thapsigargin and (<i>e</i>) thapsivillosin (<i>c</i>) Peak C could not be identified</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div> <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig.6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><img aria-describedby="Fig6" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure 6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="865"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Mass spectra from the analysis of thapsigargins by UPLC analysis of extracts of <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro plants grown in TIBs. Peak A: nortrilobolide and peak D: thapsigargin in negative mode, including the chemical structure of the two compounds</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 6" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene expression</h3><p>RNA transcription levels varied among treatments for the genes <i>TgCYP76AE2</i> (<i>p</i>&lt;0.05; F-value=4.3) and <i>TgTPS2</i> (<i>p</i>&lt;0.01; F-value=7.5) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig7">7</a>c, d). The <i>TgCYP76AE2</i> gene expression increased 1.1 fold in MS and 1.7 fold in MS+400M MeJ treatments, whereas the <i>TgTPS2</i> gene expression increased 1.8 and 3.2 fold, respectively. <i>HMGR</i> and <i>FPPS</i> expression levels did not significantly change with treatments, which shows a tight regulation of these two genes (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig7">7</a>a, b).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig.7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><img aria-describedby="Fig7" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure 7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="473"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Relative quantification of <i>HMGR</i> (<b>a</b>), <i>FPPS</i>(<b>b</b>), <i>TgTPS2</i> (<b>d</b>)and <i>TgCYP7AE2</i> (<b>c</b>)expression (qRT-PCR) with three internal controls (actin, tubulin and ef1) in <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro shoots grown in TIBs. The cultivation was performed in MS and MS+400M MeJA and the RNA was extracted after 18days of growth in TIBs. As controls TIBs with MS medium supplemented with BAP (1.5mg/L) and NAA (0.5mg/L) were used. The different letters on the bars indicate significant differences based on an ANOVA test followed by a Tukeys multiple comparison test (<i>p</i>0.05). n=9 bioreactors per treatment</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 7" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Histological analysis of explants</h3><p>Secretory ducts were observed to occur in the mid-rib of leaf cross-sections. The stem cross-sections revealed that the stems are surrounded by nested leaf sheaths, containing a large number of secretory ducts (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig8">8</a>d). The secretory ducts are surrounded by epithelial cells and radiate out from the pith of the stems to the surrounding leaf sheaths in concentric circles. The Nadi staining (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig8">8</a>) indicates that the epithelial cells and the secretory ducts contain terpenoids (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig8">8</a>e), shown by the blue color. Positive staining was also observed in the epidermis of leaves (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig8">8</a>a, b) and stems (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig8">8</a>c, d).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig.8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig.8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig8_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig8" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13007-018-0346-z/MediaObjects/13007_2018_346_Fig8_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="911"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p><b>a</b><b>f</b> NADI staining of <i>T. garganica</i> leaf and stem sections. Blue color indicates the presence of oxygenated or lipophilic compounds (e.g. terpenoids), concentrated mainly in epithelial cells, secretory ducts and in the epidermis. <b>a, b</b> Leaf cross-sections with staining visible in the epidermis. <b>c</b> upper-stem cross-section with the epidermis stained. <b>d</b> cross-section of the stem, just above callus tissue with staining concentrated in secretory ducts. <b>e</b> cross-sections of callus tissue at the base of the stems. Staining is visible in epithelial cells and secretory ducts. <b>f</b> transverse section of callus tissue showing staining of the epithelial cells along a secretory duct. <b>g, h</b> Unstained cross-section (g) and transverse section (h) of the stem to illustrate un-ruptured secretory ducts filled with resin/oil bodies <i>c</i> cortex, <i>e</i> epidermis (<i>le</i> lower epidermis, <i>ue</i> upper epidermis), <i>ep</i> epithelial cells, <i>o</i> oil bodies, <i>sd</i> secretory ducts, <i>st</i> stomata, <i>vb</i> vascular bundle. Scale bars represent 100m</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 8" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
</div></div></section><section data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The joint action of auxins and cytokinins plays a critical role in the activation of somatic cells and cell division. This has been observed previously in <i>T. garganica</i> and other species belonging to the Apiaceae family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ignacimuthu S, Arockiasamy S, Antonysamy M, Ravichandran P. Plant regeneration through somatic embryogenesis from mature leaf explants of Eryngium foetidum, a condiment. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 1999;56:1317." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2842">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Martin KP. Plant regeneration through somatic embryogenesis in medicinally important Centella asiatica L. Vitr Cell Dev Biol. 2004;40:58691." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2842_1">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hasani B, Saboura A, Rajabian T, Falah HH. Somatic embryogenesis of Ferula assa-foetida. J Sci (University Tehran). 2008;33:1523." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2842_2">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roozbeh S, Otroshy M, Bozorgipoor R, Ebrahimi M, Moeini Najafabadi A, Struik PC. Micropropagation of Ferula assa-foetida L. (a medicinal plant) via direct somatic embryogenesis. In: International symposium on medicinal and aromatic plants IMAPS2010 and history of mayan ethnopharmacology IMAPS2011 964. 2011. p. 14352." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2842_3">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Abd-Allah RH, Zeid EMA, Zakaria MM, Eldahmy SI. Micropropagation of wild fennel (Foeniculum vulgare var. vulgare) via organogenesis and somatic embryogenesis. J Dev Biol Tissue Eng. 2015;7:110." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2845">26</a>]. Therefore, our results confirm that the cytokinin and 2,4-D combination is fundamental for <i>T. garganica</i> regeneration via somatic cells. Additionally, low concentrations of plant growth regulators gave rise to the highest numbers for somatic embryogenesis. Similar results have been reported for <i>Ferula assa</i>-<i>foetida</i> L. (also an Apiaceae), where direct and indirect embryogenesis occurred in low concentrations of 2,4-D (0.5mg/L) and kinetin (0.2mg/L) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Roozbeh S, Otroshy M, Bozorgipoor R, Ebrahimi M, Moeini Najafabadi A, Struik PC. Micropropagation of Ferula assa-foetida L. (a medicinal plant) via direct somatic embryogenesis. In: International symposium on medicinal and aromatic plants IMAPS2010 and history of mayan ethnopharmacology IMAPS2011 964. 2011. p. 14352." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2858">25</a>]. According to George et al., in most plant species, a low concentration of cytokinin stimulates the initiation of embryogenic cultures. In contrast, a low concentration of auxin triggers the division of the pro-embryogenic cells and their development into embryos [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="George EF, Hall MA, De Klerk G-J. Plant propagation by tissue culture: volume 1, the background. Berlin: Springer; 2008." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2861">27</a>]. Other studies on Apiaceae species have found that embryogenic cultures in darkness are more competent than if grown under a photoperiod [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Martin KP. Plant regeneration through somatic embryogenesis in medicinally important Centella asiatica L. Vitr Cell Dev Biol. 2004;40:58691." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2864">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Michler CH, Lineberger RD. Effects of light on somatic embryo development and abscisic levels in carrot suspension cultures. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 1987;11:189207." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2867">28</a>]. In the only report related to somatic embryogenesis for <i>T. garganica,</i> the authors observed that four-year-old suspension cultures spontaneously induced embryos both in darkness and in light without specifying which condition was better [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jger A, Schottlnder B, Smitt U, Nyman U. Somatic embryogenesis in cell cultures of Thapsia garganica. Plant Cell Rep. 1993;12:51720. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/bf00236099&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2874">14</a>].</p><p>In our explants, treatment with TDZ led to scarce callus formation. TDZ has cytokinin-like activity and can stimulate de novo synthesis of auxin by increasing the levels of 2-(1H-indol-3-yl) acetic acid (IAA) and its precursors [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Landi L, Mezzetti B. TDZ, auxin and genotype effects on leaf organogenesis in Fragaria. Plant Cell Rep. 2006;25:2818." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2880">29</a>]. In <i>Fragaria</i> leaf explants, treatment with TDZ led to scarce callus formation and proliferation, and shoots became necrotic or died after 60days [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Landi L, Mezzetti B. TDZ, auxin and genotype effects on leaf organogenesis in Fragaria. Plant Cell Rep. 2006;25:2818." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2886">29</a>]. Thus, the suggested de novo synthesis of auxin is not sufficient to obtain the activation. Our observations confirm that auxin and TDZ should be combined in order to obtain viable cultures.</p><p>Previous work on <i>T. garganica</i> root induction has shown a low percentage of rooted plantlets (32%) in plant growth regulator-free medium [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Makunga NP, Jger AK, van Staden J. Micropropagation of Thapsia garganica: a medicinal plant. Plant Cell Rep. 2003;21:96773. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00299-003-0623-8&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2895">15</a>] and no roots when treated with auxins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Makunga NP, Jger AK, van Staden J. An improved system for the in vitro regeneration of Thapsia garganica via direct organogenesis: influence of auxins and cytokinins. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2005;82:27180." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2898">16</a>]. Later, 60% rooting frequency was obtained by treatment of 10mg/L 1H-indole-3-butanoic acid (IBA) for 3days prior to transfer to plant growth regulators-free medium [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Makunga NP, Jger AK, van Staden J. Improved in vitro rooting and hyperhydricity in regenerating tissues of Thapsia garganica L. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2006;86:7786." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2901">17</a>]. Here we observe that 4971% of the <i>T. garganica</i> shoots established roots in MS media in all auxin concentrations. The positive effect of MS on in vitro root induction has been previously reported for various Apiaceae species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Makunga NP, Jger AK, van Staden J. Improved in vitro rooting and hyperhydricity in regenerating tissues of Thapsia garganica L. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2006;86:7786." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2908">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Karuppusamy S, Kiranmai C, Aruna V, Pullaiah T. Micropropagation of Vanasushava pedata: an endangered medicinal plant of South India. Plant Tissue Cult Biotechnol. 2006;16:8594." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2911">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Karuppusamy S, Aruna V, Kiranmai C, Pullaiah T. In vitro propagation of an endemic umbellifer, Hydrocotyle conferta. Indian J Biotechnol. 2007;6:5414." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2911_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Purohit S, Kothari SL. Direct somatic embryogenesis from cotyledon and cotyledonary node explants in bishops weed Trachyspermum ammi (L.) Sprague. Vitr Cell Dev Biol. 2007;43:1548." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2911_2">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Irvani N, Solouki M, Omidi M, Zare AR, Shahnazi S. Callus induction and plant regeneration in Dorem ammoniacum D., an endangered medicinal plant. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2010;100:2939." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2911_3">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zare AR, Solouki M, Omidi M, Irvani N, Nezad NM, Rezazadeh S. Callus induction and plant regeneration in Ferula assafoetida L. (Asafetida), an endangered medicinal plant. Trakia J Sci. 2010;8:118." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2911_4">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Aygun A, Dumanoglu H. In vitro shoot proliferation and in vitro and ex vitro root formation of Pyrus elaeagrifolia Pallas. Front Plant Sci. 2015;6:225. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2015.00225&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2911_5">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ye S, Cai C, Ren H, Wang W, Xiang M, Tang X, Zhu C, Yin T, Zhang L, Zhu Q. An efficient plant regeneration and transformation system of Ma Bamboo (Dendrocalamus latiflorus Munro) started from young shoot as explant. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:1298." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2914">36</a>]. It is known that water or nutrient deficiencies act as powerful stimulants for the activation of cell division in root meristem, in order to capture more nutrients [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Hirai MY, Saito K. Post-genomics approaches for the elucidation of plant adaptive mechanisms to sulphur deficiency. J Exp Bot. 2004;55:18719. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/jxb/erh184&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2917">37</a>]. In addition, low concentrations of nutrients provide a low osmotic pressure and ease the adaptation of the plants during acclimation. The natural auxins IAA and IBA, and the synthetic auxin 2-(1-naphthyl)acetic acid (NAA) are the most used plant growth regulators for root induction, with IBA being the most efficient auxin for Apiaceae species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Irvani N, Solouki M, Omidi M, Zare AR, Shahnazi S. Callus induction and plant regeneration in Dorem ammoniacum D., an endangered medicinal plant. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2010;100:2939." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2920">33</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zare AR, Solouki M, Omidi M, Irvani N, Nezad NM, Rezazadeh S. Callus induction and plant regeneration in Ferula assafoetida L. (Asafetida), an endangered medicinal plant. Trakia J Sci. 2010;8:118." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2923">34</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Sharma RK, Wakhlu AK, Boleria M. Micropropagation of Anethum graveolens L. through axillary shoot proliferation. J plant Biochem. Biotechnology. 2004;13:1579." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2927">38</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Joshi K, Chaturvedi P, Shubhpriya S. Efficient in vitro regeneration protocol of Centella asiatica (L.) Urban: an endemic and underutilized nutraceutical herb. Afr J Biotechnol. 2013;12:516472." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2930">39</a>]. However, for <i>T. garganica</i>, NAA induced the highest number of roots per rooted shoot (12.052.08) as has also been observed for <i>Hydrocotyle conferta</i> (Apiaceae) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Karuppusamy S, Aruna V, Kiranmai C, Pullaiah T. In vitro propagation of an endemic umbellifer, Hydrocotyle conferta. Indian J Biotechnol. 2007;6:5414." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2939">31</a>]. HPLC analyses confirmed that 1mg/L IAA was the best auxin for thapsigargin production in both shoot and root systems, but high concentrations of auxins damaged shoot systems. <i>T. garganica</i> shoot culture controls devoid of auxins did not show root induction. Surprisingly, these shoot cultures reached the highest thapsigargin levels, even more than shoots growing in the shoot multiplication media. Hence, a reduced nutrient supply by MS was used in the following experiments.</p><p>Reduced nutrient supply, via MS media in in vitro shoots in TIBs, yielded a 1.9 fold increase of thapsigargin content compared to full MS media(Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig4">4</a>a). As a response to nutrient stress, plants often produce higher amounts of specialized metabolites and growth is retarded, to increase the survival rate of the individual [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Akula R, Ravishankar GA, Ramakrishna A, Ravishankar GA. Influence of abiotic stress signals on secondary metabolites in plants. Plant Signal Behav. 2011;6:172031." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2951">40</a>]. As observed here, nutrient stress has been shown to significantly increase levels of the sesquiterpene lactone artemisinin in <i>Artemisia</i> cells whilst causing a reduction in plant biomass [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Ferreira JFS. Nutrient deficiency in the production of artemisinin, dihydroartemisinic acid, and artemisinic acid in Artemisia annua L. J Agric Food Chem. 2007;55:168694." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2957">41</a>].</p><p>Elicitors have been used to either increase the production or to induce de novo synthesis of secondary metabolites in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="He Y, Yan H, Hua W, Huang Y, Wang Z. Selection and validation of reference genes for quantitative real-time PCR in Gentiana macrophylla. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:945. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2016.00945&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2964">42</a>]. MeJA is a plant-specific signalling molecule synthesized in response to pathogen attack and wounding, and has been shown to be a powerful elicitor within Apiaceae species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim OT, Kim MY, Hong MH, Ahn JC, Hwang B. Stimulation of asiaticoside accumulation in the whole plant cultures of Centella asiatica (L.) Urban by elicitors. Plant Cell Rep. 2004;23:33944." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2967">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mangas S, Bonfill M, Osuna L, Moyano E, Tortoriello J, Cusido RM, Piol MT, Palazn J. The effect of methyl jasmonate on triterpene and sterol metabolisms of Centella asiatica, Ruscus aculeatus and Galphimia glauca cultured plants. Phytochemistry. 2006;67:20419." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2967_1">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kikowska M, Budzianowski J, Krawczyk A, Thiem B. Accumulation of rosmarinic, chlorogenic and caffeic acids in in vitro cultures of Eryngium planum L. Acta Physiol Plant. 2012;34:242533." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2967_2">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ruslan K, Selfitri AD, Bulan SA, Rukayadi Y. Effect of Agrobacterium rhizogenes and elicitation on the asiaticoside production in cell cultures of Centella asiatica. Pharmacogn Mag. 2012;8:111." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2967_3">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kikowska M, Kdziora J, Krawczyk A, Thiem B. Methyl jasmonate, yeast extract and sucrose stimulate phenolic acids accumulation in Eryngium planum L. shoot cultures. Acta Biochim Pol. 2015;62:1972000." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2967_4">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ruan J-X, Li J-X, Fang X, Wang L-J, Hu W-L, Chen X-Y, Yang C-Q. Isolation and characterization of three new monoterpene synthases from Artemisia annua. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:638. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2016.00638&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2967_5">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Cai J, Ma Y, Hu P, Zhang Y, Chen J, Li X. Elicitation of furanocoumarins in Changium smyrnioides suspension cells. Plant Cell, Tissue Organ Cult. 2017;130:112." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2970">49</a>]. MeJA stimulates a stress response that often leads to the biosynthesis of specialized metabolites [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ramirez-Estrada K, Vidal-Limon H, Hidalgo D, Moyano E, Golenioswki M, Cusid RM, Palazon J. Elicitation, an effective strategy for the biotechnological production of bioactive high-added value compounds in plant cell factories. Molecules. 2016;21:182." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2973">50</a>], such as terpenoids. The application of MeJA to <i>T. garganica</i> shoot cultures grown in TIBs significantly increased nortrilobolide biosynthesis, leading to a 2.5 fold increase after 18days of treatments with 400M MeJA(Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig3">3</a>b). Similar results were obtained for root cultures of <i>Hyoscyamus muticus</i> L., where the sesquiterpene production was enhanced by MeJA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Singh G, Gavrieli J, Oakey JS, Curtis WR. Interaction of methyl jasmonate, wounding and fungal elicitation during sesquiterpene induction in Hyoscyamus muticus in root cultures. Plant Cell Rep. 1998;17:3915." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2986">51</a>].</p><p>The joint treatment with MS and MeJA yielded very high concentrations of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide in <i>T. garganica</i> shoots grown in TIBs, a very promising result despite the reduced biomass(Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig4">4</a>). The combination of the treatment and the TIB cultivation can provide a sustainable and viable production of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide. Additionally, nortrilobolide can easily be converted into thapsigargin or other relevant drug precursors by a 3-step chemical synthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Doan NTQ, Crestey F, Olsen CE, Christensen SB.
