Chemo- and regioselective functionalization of nortrilobolide: application for semisynthesis of the natural product 2-acetoxytrilobolide. J Nat Prod. 2015;78:140614. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1021/acs.jnatprod.5b00333&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e2998">52</a>].</p><p>In plants, sesquiterpenoids are mainly biosynthesized by the mevalonate pathway, where HMGR and FPPS are considered key enzymes in the regulation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Drew DP, Rasmussen SK, Avato P, Simonsen HT. A comparison of headspace solid-phase microextraction and classic hydrodistillation for the identification of volatile constituents from Thapsia spp. provides insights into guaianolide biosynthesis in Apiaceae. Phytochem Anal. 2012;23:4451. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1002/pca.1323&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3004">53</a>]. The first specific step in the biosynthesis of sesquiterpenoids is catalyzed by sesquiterpene synthases followed by modifications in the backbone skeleton by cytochromes P450 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Weitzel C, Simonsen HT. Cytochrome P450-enzymes involved in the biosynthesis of mono- and sesquiterpenes. Phytochem Rev. 2015;14:724. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11101-013-9280-x&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3007">54</a>]. Two sesquiterpene synthases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pickel B, Drew DP, Manczak T, Weitzel C, Simonsen HT, Ro D. Identification and characterization of a kunzeaol synthase from Thapsia garganica: implications for the biosynthesis of the pharmaceutical thapsigargin. Biochem J. 2012;448:26171. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1042/bj20120654&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3010">11</a>] and one cytochrome P450 have been described from the root transcriptome of <i>T. garganica</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Andersen TB, Martinez KA, Rasmussen SA, Boughton BA, Jrgensen K, Andersen-Ranberg J, Nyberg N, Christensen SB, Simonsen HT. Localization and in vivo characterization of Thapsia garganica CYP76AE2 indicates a role in thapsigargin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:5672. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.16.00055&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3016">12</a>]. The sesquiterpene synthase TgTPS2 is the most interesting one, with epikunzeaol being its major product [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pickel B, Drew DP, Manczak T, Weitzel C, Simonsen HT, Ro D. Identification and characterization of a kunzeaol synthase from Thapsia garganica: implications for the biosynthesis of the pharmaceutical thapsigargin. Biochem J. 2012;448:26171. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1042/bj20120654&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3020">11</a>] followed by the cytochrome TgCYP76AE2, which converts epikunzeaol to epidihydrocostunolide a likely precursor of thapsigargins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Andersen TB, Martinez KA, Rasmussen SA, Boughton BA, Jrgensen K, Andersen-Ranberg J, Nyberg N, Christensen SB, Simonsen HT. Localization and in vivo characterization of Thapsia garganica CYP76AE2 indicates a role in thapsigargin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:5672. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.16.00055&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3023">12</a>]. We observed that the amount of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide in <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro shoots exhibited a positive and significant correlation with the expression of <i>TgCYP76AE2</i> and <i>TgTPS2</i> genes. Although the levels of expression of <i>TgHMGR</i> and <i>TgFPPS</i> did not change(Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig7">7</a>a,b), the increase in the expression of <i>TgCYP76AE2</i> and <i>TgTPS2</i> genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig7">7</a>c,d)and the levels of sesquiterpenoids correlate with what was observed in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana.</i> Here the sesquiterpene synthases TPS21 and TPS11 were induced by jasmonate, leading to an increased emission of sesquiterpenes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Hong G-J, Xue X-Y, Mao Y-B, Wang L-J, Chen X-Y. Arabidopsis MYC2 interacts with DELLA proteins in regulating sesquiterpene synthase gene expression. Plant Cell. 2012;24:263548." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3058">55</a>]. This shows that the levels of expression of terpene synthases and cytochromes P450 part of terpenoid biosynthesis in <i>Thapsia</i> can be induced by stress factors, and our data supports that TgTPS2 and TgCYP76AE2 are likely to be involved in thapsigargin biosynthesis.</p><p>The histochemical analysis with Nadi staining enabled the identification of secretory ducts within the stem, leaves and callus tissue of the in vitro plant material, whereas previous work focused on only root sections of <i>T. garganica</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Andersen TB, Martinez KA, Rasmussen SA, Boughton BA, Jrgensen K, Andersen-Ranberg J, Nyberg N, Christensen SB, Simonsen HT. Localization and in vivo characterization of Thapsia garganica CYP76AE2 indicates a role in thapsigargin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:5672. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.16.00055&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3070">12</a>]. As previously reported [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Andersen TB, Martinez KA, Rasmussen SA, Boughton BA, Jrgensen K, Andersen-Ranberg J, Nyberg N, Christensen SB, Simonsen HT. Localization and in vivo characterization of Thapsia garganica CYP76AE2 indicates a role in thapsigargin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:5672. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.16.00055&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3073">12</a>], these secretory ducts were surrounded by epithelial cells and the Nadi staining, together with the HPLC results, provided a good indication for the presence of terpenoids and hence thapsigargins in these specialised structures. Resin exudation is known to be regulated by the epithelial cells, being used by plants to store specialised metabolites to prevent auto-toxicity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Taiz L, Zeiger E, Mller IM, Murphy A. Plant physiology and development. 6th ed. Oxford: Oxford University Press; 2015." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3076">56</a>]. The relatively large number of secretory ducts observed in the stems and callus tissue of the explants could explain the high quantity of thapsigargin measured in the HPLC analyses. It remains to be shown whether the number of ducts can be correlated with the stress factors and the expression of the terpenoid related genes, as the current data did not allow for this conclusion. Likewise, our data indicate that an induction of duct formation might be a strategy towards higher production of specialized metabolites in plants.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>We have shown that <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro tissue culture is an efficient production platform of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide. The in vitro culture can be formed from leaf explants. The overall setup can supply thapsigargin for commercial and pharmacological needs at industrial levels. Further process engineering is needed to optimize <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro shoot biomass production and thapsigargin levels. Our technology and method can protect this medicinal plant from future over-harvesting, as well as establishing the prerequisite cultures for plant transformation. Finally, we have confirmed that higher expression levels of <i>TgTPS2</i> and <i>TgCYP76AE2</i> genes lead to higher thapsigargin content, thus supporting their likely involvement in thapsigargin biosynthesis.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">In vitro culture establishment</h3><p><i>Thapsia garganica</i> L. plant material was obtained from a two-year-old stock plant growing under greenhouse conditions (University of Copenhagen, Denmark). Leaves were collected to be used as explants, washed with a detergent solution and rinsed under running tap water to remove loose dirt. For surface sterilization, the explants were dipped in a 0.5% sodium hypochlorite solution with tween 20<sup></sup> for 15min, followed by three rinses with sterile distilled water. Leaf segments were aseptically inoculated, abaxial-side down, on Petri dishes containing 25ml of Murashige and Skoog (MS) salts and vitamins (Duchefa Biochemie, The Netherlands), 30g/L sucrose, 0.25g/L phytagel and supplemented with three different plant growth regulators, N-(Phenylmethyl)-7H-purin-6-amine (BAP) or 1-phenyl-3-(1,2,3-thiadiazol-5-yl) urea (TDZ) (0.1, 0.5 or 1mg/L), either alone or in combination with 2,4-Dichlorophenoxyacetic acid (2,4-D) (0.1mg/L). Cultures were incubated under a 16h light photoperiod or 24h darkness. Each treatment consisted of 15 explants spread over three Petri dishes and the experiment was repeated twice (n=30). Shoot organogenesis and somatic embryogenesis were investigated after culturing for a total of 12weeks. The induction of shoots, callus, and somatic embryos was observed monthly (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Tab1">1</a>). The different treatments gave rise to different callus types. Type I: friable, soft white calli; type II: nodular green organogenetic and compact calli; type III: creamy, yellow nodular calli; type IV: brown dead calli. Furthermore, the amount of calli was noted from low to high (, +, ++, +++).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Culture media and incubation conditions</h3><p>For all culture media, the pH was adjusted to 5.8 before autoclaving at 121C and 103kPa for 20min.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Culture establishment media</h4><p>The medium was MS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Murashige T, Skoog F. A revised medium for rapid growth and bioassays with tobacco tissue culture. Physiol Plant. 1962;15:47397." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3133">57</a>] salts and vitamins (Duchefa Biochemie, The Netherlands), 30g/L sucrose, 0.25g/L phytagel (Duchefa Biochemie, The Netherlands). This was supplemented with three different plant growth regulators (plant growth regulators), BAP or TDZ (0.1, 0.5 or 1mg/L), either alone or in combination with 2,4-D (0.1mg/L). TDZ was dissolved in NaOH and filter-sterilized through a 0.24M filter and added to autoclaved medium after it had cooled while the rest of plant growth regulators were added prior to autoclaving. Cultures were incubated in Petri dishes at 232C under a 16h light photoperiod (15molm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>1</sup>) or 24h darkness.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">Shoot multiplication medium</h4><p>After three months, <i>T. garganica</i> shoots, buds and embryos regenerated from the leaflet explants grown in the different culture establishment media were transferred to a shoot multiplication medium defined as MS salts and vitamins, 30g/L sucrose and 7g/L plant agar (Duchefa Biochemie, The Netherlands). This was supplemented with 1.5mg/L BAP and 0.5mg/L 2-(1-naphthyl)acetic acid (NAA). Shoot cultures were multiplied in glass culture vessels, incubated at 232C under a 16h light photoperiod (15molm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>1</sup>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec17">Rooting media</h4><p>The medium was half strength MS salts (MS) with MS vitamins, 30g/L sucrose and 7g/L plant agar. This medium was supplemented with 2-(1H-indol-3-yl) acetic acid (IAA), NAA and 1H-indole-3-butanoic acid (IBA) (1-4mg/L). Cultures were incubated in 20ml culture tubes (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig2">2</a>a) at 232C under a 16h light photoperiod (15molm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>1</sup>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">TIBs setup and culture media</h4><p>1 Liter autoclavable RITA<sup></sup> bioreactors were used (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig2">2</a>bd) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Teisson C, Alvard D. A new concept of plant in vitro cultivation liquid medium: temporary immersion. In: Terzi M, Cella R, Falavigna A, editors. Current issues in plant molecular and cellular biology: proceedings of the VIIIth international congress on plant tissue and cell culture, Florence, Italy, 1217 June, 1994. Dordrecht: Springer Netherlands; 1995. p. 10510. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/978-94-011-0307-7_12&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3184">58</a>]. The RITA<sup></sup> bioreactor consists of two compartments; an upper one, where the plants are placed in a raft support; and a lower one, which contains 200mL of the culture medium. Both compartments are connected through a small tube and the raft support placed at the bottom of the upper compartment. When applying overpressure by means of the small tube, the culture medium goes up and immerses the plants in the upper compartment. During the immersion, the airflow continuously agitates and oxygenates the medium, allowing an inner atmosphere renewal. When pressure drops, the medium drains into the lower compartment by gravity. The overpressure is generated by an air pump linked to several RITA<sup></sup> bioreactors. The air pump is controlled by a timer that regulates the frequency and the length of the overpressure; the cultures were immersed for 3min every 6h. The entry and exit flows were sterilized through 0.2-m filters. The RITA<sup></sup> bioreactors and the medium were autoclaved separately before use.</p><p>For the MeJa treatment, the medium used was MS salts and vitamins and 30g/L sucrose. This was supplemented with 1.5mg/L BAP and 0.5mg/L NAA and different concentrations of MeJA (100, 200 and 400M). Cultures were incubated in TIBs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Michoux F, Ahmad N, Hennig A, Nixon PJ, Warzecha H. Production of leafy biomass using temporary immersion bioreactors: an alternative platform to express proteins in transplastomic plants with drastic phenotypes. Planta. 2013;237:9038. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00425-012-1829-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3197">13</a>] at 232C under a 16h light photoperiod (15molm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>1</sup>). Three bioreactors were used for each treatment, and the experiment was repeated three times (n=9). A sample from each bioreactor was taken after 3, 6, 12 and 18days for quantification of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide by HPLC.</p><p>For the reduced nutrient supply treatment, the medium was MS with MS vitamins salts and 30g/L sucrose. This medium was free of plant growth regulators and either supplemented with 400M MeJA (MS+400M MeJA) or without MeJA (MS). Cultures were incubated in TIBs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Michoux F, Ahmad N, Hennig A, Nixon PJ, Warzecha H. Production of leafy biomass using temporary immersion bioreactors: an alternative platform to express proteins in transplastomic plants with drastic phenotypes. Planta. 2013;237:9038. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00425-012-1829-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3207">13</a>] at 232C under a 16h light photoperiod (15molm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>1</sup>Photosynthetic Photon Flux Density). A sample from each bioreactor was taken after 18days for thapsigargin and nortrilobolide HPLC quantification and gene expression analysis by qRT-PCR of the genes of interest: <i>HMGR</i>, <i>FPPS</i>, <i>TgTPS2</i> and <i>TgCYP76AE</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Shoot multiplication</h3><p><i>Thapsia garganica</i> shoots, buds and embryos regenerated from the leaflet explants were transferred to a shoot multiplication medium plant growth regulator to induce shoot multiplication. All regenerated shoots were left to multiply and grow in culture vessels with solid medium. Shoots cultured for approximately 3weeks were transferred to TIBs with liquid medium, in order to use them as an efficient production platform system of thapsigargins.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Root formation</h3><p>Regenerated single shoots (47cm) were transferred onto rooting medium (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig2">2</a>a). After cultivation for 4weeks, the plants were transferred to MS medium free of plant growth regulators. The control was MS without plant growth regulators. Each treatment consisted of 15 shoots, and the experiment was repeated three times (n=45). The percentage of root formation and the number of roots was recorded weekly over a period of 6weeks. The content of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide was quantified by HPLC; sampling was performed after 8weeks to allow for sufficient biomass content. The shoots and roots of the generated plants in all treatments were sampled and the quantification was performed separately in these trials (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Tab2">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Enhancement of thapsigargins production in TIBs by MeJA and reduced nutrient supply</h3><p>For the treatment with MeJa, 4-7cm shoots were added into TIBs (15 shoots per TIB) containing 200mL of liquid MS medium supplemented with BAP (1.5mg/L) and NAA (0.5mg/L), and different concentrations of MeJA (100, 200 and 400M) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig2">2</a>b). TIBs with MS medium supplemented with BAP (1.5mg/L) and NAA (0.5mg/L) were used to grow controls. Three bioreactors were used for each treatment, and the experiment was repeated three times (n=9). A sample from each bioreactor was taken after 3, 6, 12 and 18days for thapsigargin and nortrilobolide HPLC quantification.</p><p>In the reduced nutrient supply treatment, 4-7cm shoots were added into TIBs (15 shoots per TIB) containing 200mL of liquid MS medium with 400M MeJA or without MeJA (MS) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#Fig2">2</a>b). Controls were cultured in MS medium supplemented with BAP (1.5mg/L) and NAA (0.5mg/L). Three bioreactors were used for each treatment, and the experiment was repeated three times (n=9). A sample from each bioreactor was taken after 18days for thapsigargin and nortrilobolide HPLC quantification and gene expression analysis by qRT-PCR of genes of interest: <i>HMGR, FPPS, TgTPS2 and TgCYP76AE</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Quantification of thapsigargin</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec23">Extraction of thapsigargin from plant material</h4><p>Plant material from in vitro cultures (shoots and roots) was frozen at 80C and dried 48h in a freeze drier (LABCONCO FreeZone 2.5 plus). 50mg of each dried sample was ground and extracted overnight with 1.5mL 70% ethanol. The extract was centrifuged at 13,000rpm for 10min. 1mL of the supernatant underwent evaporation by speed vacuum until dry and the residue was dissolved in 250L methanol 80% (concentration 4). The extracts were then filtered through a 0.45m filter, placed into 1.5mL vials and kept at 20C until HPLCDAD analysis.</p><p>Standard solutions of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide were prepared in 80% methanol in the range 121200mg/L and 111007mg/L respectively, corresponding to the concentration range of the plant extracts. Calibration curves were generated by integrating the peak area (230nm) versus the concentration of standard thapsigargins. For both, the calibration curves were linear with a correlation coefficient of 0.99. The results were converted to mg of compound per g of dry weight plant (mg/g DW). The standards used in all chromatography experiments of thapsigargin and nortrilobolide and the mass spectrums of all thapsigargins were provided by Prof. Sren B. Christensen, University of Copenhagen, Denmark.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">High-performance liquid chromatography quantification of thapsigargins</h4><p>The HPLCDAD system consisted of a quaternary pump (JASCO-2089 Plus pump), a thermoregulated autosampler set at 4C (Intelligent autosampler JASCO AS-2059) and a photodiode array detector (PDA) detector (UV/VIS detector JASCO MD-2018 Plus). The column temperature was 30C. Separation of compounds was achieved on a Luna C18 column (5m, 4.6mm25cm) (Phenomenex, USA). The flow rate was set to 0.5mL/min and the mobile phase consisted of water with <i>o</i>-phosphoric acid (0.01%) as solvent A and acetonitrile with <i>o</i>-phosphoric acid (0.01%) as solvent B. The binary gradient elution was: 80100% A (015min., linear gradient), 100% A (1525min.) and 10080% A (2527.5min., linear gradient).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec25">Identification of thapsigargins by UPLC-MS</h4><p>Qualitative analysis of thapsigargins from the in vitro plants was performed using a Waters Acquity TQD UPLC/MS/MS (triple quadrupole mass spectrometer) system associated with a PDA detector using the standards mentioned above. The mass spectrums was compared with those provided by Prof. Sren B. Christensen, University of Copenhagen, Denmark. The system was directed by the software MassLynx 4.1. Extracts were filtered at 0.2m and 7.5L of each extract was separated on a C18 Luna (2)-HST (1002.0mm, 2.5m) column (Phenomenex, USA). The flow rate was set to 0.6mL/min and the mobile phases consisted of water with o-phosphoric acid (0.01%) for A and acetonitrile with o-phosphoric acid (0.01%) for B. The binary gradient elution was: 90% A (00.5min.), 900% A (0.57min., linear gradient), 0% A (710min.), 095% A (1010.2min., linear gradient) and 95% A (10.213min.). The PDA swept wavelengths from 200 to 400nm with a 2.4 resolution. Mass spectra were obtained in positive and negative modes, using an electrospray ionization (ESI) source on a triple quadrupole instrument (Waters Acquity) in full scan (502000m/z). The conditions were as follows, capillary voltage: 3000V, cone voltages: 30 and 60V, desolvation temperature: 450C, source temperature: 150C, gas flow cone: 50l/min, desolvation gas: 800l/min.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Gene expression analysis by qRT-PCR</h3><p>RNA transcription levels of genes of interest: <i>HMGR</i>, <i>FPPS</i>, <i>TgTPS2</i> and <i>TgCYP76AE2</i> were measured for <i>T. garganica</i> in vitro shoots growing in TIBs under reduced nutrient supply (MS) or under reduced nutrient supply in combination with MeJA (MS+400M MeJA). Controls were cultured in TIBs with MS medium supplemented with BAP (1.5mg/L) and NAA (0.5mg/L).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec27">RNA isolation and primer design</h4><p>Total RNA was isolated from 75 to 85mg samples from elicited and non-elicited shoots. RNA was extracted with the Spectrum Plant Total RNA kit (Sigma-Aldrich, Copenhagen, Denmark) and was treated with DNaseI Amplification Grade (Sigma-Aldrich, Copenhagen, Denmark). To determine nucleic acid purity, 260/280nm ratios were measured using an Agilent RNA 6000 Nano kit (Agilent Technologies, Copenhagen, Denmark).</p><p>Three reference genes were selected for normalization: actin, elongation factor (ef1), and tubulin. The previously published <i>T. garganica</i> root transcriptome data (SRX096991) was used for primer design. <i>Daucus carota</i> sequences, acquired from NCBI database, of genes: actin, tubulin, <i>FPP</i> and <i>HMGR</i> were used to do a BLAST against <i>T. garganica</i> root transcriptome, while <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> sequence was used for ef1 gene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Andersen TB, Martinez KA, Rasmussen SA, Boughton BA, Jrgensen K, Andersen-Ranberg J, Nyberg N, Christensen SB, Simonsen HT. Localization and in vivo characterization of Thapsia garganica CYP76AE2 indicates a role in thapsigargin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:5672. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.16.00055&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3363">12</a>]. <i>Daucus</i> is the sister genus to <i>Thapsia</i> thus was an obvious choice for the design of primers. For the ef1 <i>Solanum</i> was the closest relative available. Primer design of <i>TgCYP76AE2</i> and <i>TgTPS2</i> was based on the published sequences [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pickel B, Drew DP, Manczak T, Weitzel C, Simonsen HT, Ro D. Identification and characterization of a kunzeaol synthase from Thapsia garganica: implications for the biosynthesis of the pharmaceutical thapsigargin. Biochem J. 2012;448:26171. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1042/bj20120654&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3382">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Andersen TB, Martinez KA, Rasmussen SA, Boughton BA, Jrgensen K, Andersen-Ranberg J, Nyberg N, Christensen SB, Simonsen HT. Localization and in vivo characterization of Thapsia garganica CYP76AE2 indicates a role in thapsigargin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:5672. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.16.00055&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3385">12</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#MOESM1">1</a>: TableS1).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec28">Two steps qRT-PCR</h4><p>A total of 500ng of each RNA sample was reverse transcribed to cDNA with IScript cDNA Synthesis kit (BIO-RAD, Copenhagen, Denmark). Two-step qRT-PCR (as specified by the kit supplier) was performed using QuantiFast<sup></sup> SYBR<sup></sup> Green PCR (Qiagen, Copenhagen, Denmark) according to the manufacturers protocol. The following amplification program was used: 95C for 5min, 40 cycles at 95C for 10s followed by 60C for 30s. Samples were amplified in duplicate from the same RNA isolation. qRT-PCRs were performed using 3 biological replicates from different bioreactors and the experiment was repeated 3 times. Therefore, 9 biological replicates (with 18 data points) spread out in three different elicitation experiments were analyzed.</p><p>qRT-PCR efficiency, E, was estimated for each gene by generating standard curves by plotting quantification cycle (C<sub>q</sub>) values (y) against the log of a series of cDNA dilutions (x). For this, a cDNA sample was used as template in a range of 20, 4, 2, 1 and 0.5ng. The qRT-PCR efficiencies were calculated from the slope (a) of the linear regression equations of the standard curves, along with the regression coefficient (R<sup>2</sup>). The equation used was: E=10<sup>(1/a)</sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Rasmussen R. Quantification on the LightCycler. In: Meuer S, Wittwer C, Nakagawara KI, editors. Rapid cycle real-time PCR, methods and applications. Berlin: Springer; 2001. p. 2134." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3412">59</a>]. E values in a range of 1.902.10 (PCR efficiency between 90 and 110%) with a regression coefficient below 0.02 are acceptable. All PCR efficiencies displayed between 96 and 104% (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#MOESM1">1</a>: TableS1).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec29">Determination of reference gene expression stability, data analysis and normalization using geNorm</h4><p>The stability of the reference genes and normalization factors were evaluated with geNorm V.3.1 (<a href="http://www.genorm.cmgg.be">www.genorm.cmgg.be</a>). The geNorm algorithm is based on the pairwise variation of a single reference candidate gene relative to all other genes, assuming that the expression ratios of the two ideal reference genes are identical in all samples regardless of the conditions tested [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Vandesompele J, De Preter K, Pattyn F, Poppe B, Van Roy N, De Paepe A, Speleman F. Accurate normalization of real-time quantitative RT-PCR data by geometric averaging of multiple internal control genes. Genome Biol. 2002;3:research0034.1research0034.11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3433">60</a>]. geNorm calculates two parameters: the expression stability value of reference gene (M) and the coefficient of variation of the normalized reference gene relative quantities (CV) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Hellemans J, Mortier G, De Paepe A, Speleman F, Vandesompele J. qBase relative quantification framework and software for management and automated analysis of real-time quantitative PCR data. Genome Biol. 2007;8:R19." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3436">61</a>]. The cut-off proposed for typical stable reference genes for M and CV values are 0.5 and 0.25 respectively, however, for more heterogeneous samples, M and CV values can increase to 1 and 0.5 respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Hellemans J, Mortier G, De Paepe A, Speleman F, Vandesompele J. qBase relative quantification framework and software for management and automated analysis of real-time quantitative PCR data. Genome Biol. 2007;8:R19." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3439">61</a>].</p><p>Normalization factors were calculated following the --Ct method as previously described [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Livak KJ, Schmittgen TD. Analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the 2CT method. Methods. 2001;25:4028." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3445">62</a>] and using a reference target normalization approach with the three reference genes: actin, elongation factor (ef1), and tubulin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Vandesompele J, De Preter K, Pattyn F, Poppe B, Van Roy N, De Paepe A, Speleman F. Accurate normalization of real-time quantitative RT-PCR data by geometric averaging of multiple internal control genes. Genome Biol. 2002;3:research0034.1research0034.11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3448">60</a>].</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec30">Verification of amplified products and sequencing reactions</h4><p>PCR product size was checked on a 2% agarose gel and melting curves showed a singled product for all genes. To verify sequences of amplification products, PCRs for each primer set were performed. PCR reactions comprised: 0.5M of primers, 0.02 U/l of Phusion High-Fidelity DNA Polymerase (Thermo Scientific, Copenhagen, Denmark), 2mM of dNTP mix, 1Phusion HF Buffer (Thermo Scientific, Copenhagen, Denmark) and 10ng cDNA in a total volume of 20l. The following amplification program was used: 98C for 30s and 34 cycles at 98C for 5s, 58C for10s and 72C for 20s followed by 72C for 5min. Amplified products were cloned with CloneJET PCR Cloning Kit (Thermo Scientific, Copenhagen, Denmark) for sequencing, according to the manufacturers instructions. <i>E. coli</i> strain (DH5) was transformed with the ligation products, 4 colonies for each gene were analyzed by colony PCR with plasmid reverse specific primer (5-AAGAACATCGATTTTCCATGGCAG-3) at an annealing temperature of 60C. One colony per gene was selected for sequencing (Eurofins), previous plasmid preparation (GenElute Plasmid Miniprep kit, Sigma-Aldrich, Copenhagen, Denmark). Sequences of amplification products were compared through a BLAST analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec31">Histological analysis of explants</h3><p>A histological analysis was performed to investigate the tissue structure of the in vitro shoots, and a histochemical analysis with Nadi was carried out to identify secretory structures storing terpenoids. Nadi staining has been described as a tool to detect terpenoids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="David R, Carde JP. Coloration diffrentielle des inclusions lipidique et terpeniques des pseudophylles du Pin maritime au moyen du reactif Nadi. Comptes Rendus lAcadmie des Sci Paris D. 1964;258:133840." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3472">63</a>] and has been used to this effect in a number of studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ibanez S, Dtterl S, Anstett M, Baudino S, Caissard J, Gallet C, Desprs L. The role of volatile organic compounds, morphology and pigments of globeflowers in the attraction of their specific pollinating flies. New Phytol. 2010;188:45163." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3475">64</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kuster VC, Vale FHA. Leaf histochemistry analysis of four medicinal species from Cerrado. Rev Bras Farm J Pharmacogn. 2016;26:6738." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3478">65</a>]. The method is a reaction that is believed to result from the oxidation of naphthalen-1-ol and N,N-dimethylbenzene-1,4-diamine by cytochrome oxidase enzymes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Harwig J. The use of histochemical reagents for cytochrome oxidase in plant tissues. Bot Rev. 1967;33:11629. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02858666&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3481">66</a>]. In the presence of monoterpenes and sesquiterpenes, the diamine is enzymatically oxidised, resulting in a free radical that combines with naphthalen-1-ol to form 4-(4-hydroxyphenyl)iminocyclohexa-2,5-dien-1-one blue [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Harwig J. The use of histochemical reagents for cytochrome oxidase in plant tissues. Bot Rev. 1967;33:11629. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02858666&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3484">66</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Demarco D. Histochemical analysis of plant secretory structures. In: Pellicciari C, Biggiogera M, editors. Histochemistry of single molecules. Methods in molecular biology. New York: Humana Press; 2017. p. 31330." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3488">67</a>]. For the histological analysis, 65m sections of the stems, leaves and callus tissue were made using a vibratome (Microtome, HM 650V vibratome (Thermo Scientific). The sections were collected in tubes containing either 0.05M phosphate buffer (pH 7.2), to keep unstained, or in Nadi reagent (1% naphthalen-1-ol with 1% N,N-Dimethylbenzene-1,4-diamine in 0.05M phosphate buffer, pH 7.2; immersed for approximately 30min). The sections were then mounted on glass slides and observed under a Leica DMR HC microscope through 5, 10, 20, 40 dry objectives and a 100 oil immersion objective. The resulting images were processed and analysed using the Fiji platform on ImageJ [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Schindelin J, Arganda-Carreras I, Frise E, Kaynig V, Longair M, Pietzsch T, Preibisch S, Rueden C, Saalfeld S, Schmid B. Fiji: an open-source platform for biological-image analysis. Nat Methods. 2012;9:67682." href="/articles/10.1186/s13007-018-0346-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d142365605e3491">68</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec32">Statistical analysis</h3><p>All experiments were repeated 3 times, except for the regeneration from <i>Thapsia</i> leaves that was repeated twice. The data are reported as meanSE (standard error) including all data points. Means were subjected to a one-way analysis of variance (ANOVA) test followed by a Tukeys multiple comparison test (<i>p</i>0.05).</p></div></div></section>
